The sledge hammer bouncing off the window is something I have seen in person. A friend of mine explained it as an intended feature of safety glass. It's designed to take straight-on blunt collisions. But, if you chip it with some like the chipped edges of a ceramic spark plug, the jagged edge is able to slip into the "lattice" of the safety glass and cause a chain reaction that causes it to break away. Protect in most collisions, but give way when you need to pull someone out of a burning wreck.

Hahaha! Jeez Gav ... whenever someone told you that you can break a car window with a spark plug what they meant was that you can break a car window with _the ceramic_ from a spark plug. LOL You don't throw the whole hoofin' spark plug through the window! The plug is just a source of ceramic is all. Do this one again please ... take a spark plug, smash the ceramic on it, tie a little piece of the ceramic onto a string and then swing that little piece at toughened glass - when one of the sharp corners/sharp edges of the ceramic catches the glass just right the glass should shatter completely without caving in. At 3:03 flipping between the frames where the spark plug impacts, you can see that while the plug itself moves across less than a full pixel, the cracks in the glass are already propagating so fast that the latter frame they're already about the width of the plug away from the point of impact. It never ceases to astound me how fast glass breaks, it's mesmerising and mind boggling in such a beautiful way.  Edit: to put it into numbers, between 6:09 and 6:13 the crack moves from about 2/3rds of the way across the frame at 12:10:12.822308.86 to the left edge at 12:10:12.822504.86, meaning it propagated across that distance (which looks to be a few cm) over the span of 296Œºs, or about 67-100 times faster than chemical signals racing through your neurons to process sensory information (which takes about 20-30ms). In other words, the time between the first photons carrying information about the fact that a large pane of glass is breaking hitting your retina and your brain having turned that into a single frame image, the crack will have advanced by at least 30 meters - and that is before you've started thinking about what that new information means. Granted, the paper I found doesn't specify what kind of glass they used, but at room temperature the speeds at which most kinds of glass break aren't going to be significantly different.  Sources: _Glass-fracture velocity_ by F. E. Barstow and H. E. Edgerton ( https://ceramics.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/j.1151-2916.1939.tb19471.x ) _How fast is the speed of thought?_ by Martin J. Tov√©e ( https://www.science.smith.edu/departments/neurosci/courses/bio330/pdf/94CurrBiolTovee.pdf )

I did some rough calculations from the Twirly Whirlies in the extended edition of The Hobbit: Battle of Five Armies.  An average longbow arrow is 2.5184‚Äô long and travels at 160 FPS. An average ballista bolt travels at 186 FPS. This gives a convergence rate of 346 FPS. Let‚Äôs say these things are designed to hit an arrow halfway along its length to at least knock it off course. This means that for every 1.2592‚Äô (half an arrow length) the ballista bolt and arrow converge (at 346 FPS), whatever is rotating on the ballista bolt needs to move through the space of any potential arrow. If only one ‚Äòwire‚Äô was being used to cut the arrows down, this would translate to 16,487 RPM on the ballista bolt. With something like 8 wires, which is a bit more likely, we‚Äôd only need 2,061 RPM.  Now for the rotation speed at the tips of the wires. I count 200 elves in the archer row. With an average back-to-chest length of an average male being ~9.5‚Äù and with a 20-inch back-to-front spacing between archers, each archer takes up 29.5‚Äù of space. This gives us somewhere around 491‚Äô that the twirly whirlies need to cover, at minimum. We see 9 total twirly whirlies fired, and by my estimate, about 1.3 of them do not impact any arrows and are simply there for coverage. That means that 491‚Äô is covered by ~7.7 twirly whirlies, giving each one a diameter of 63.9‚Äô. This gives us a twirly whirly circumference of 200.646‚Äô. Multiply that number by our 8-wire RPM to get a 413,531 FPM speed at the tips of our wires. That‚Äôs 4,699 mph at the tips.  Now for reference, every time we double the number of wires, we can halve the rotation speed. So if you think 8 ‚Äòwires‚Äô may be too few or too many, the math is fairly simple to do if you‚Äôd like to add or remove wires. My question for you guys is: How fast can wood spin before it shatters? Beyond whatever magical way the dwarves could get the Twirly Whirlies spinning that fast, could a wooden ballista bolt shaft even withstand rotating at those thousands of RPM? What speed does wood shatter at? I remember you guys did something very similar with a CD and I‚Äôd love to see if you could come up with something for this!

3:03 If you go frame-by-frame, you can see that some cracks actually go backwards -- toward the source.   Once the initial run of cracking has passed, there are some 'bald spots' (for lack of a better term) that are formed while it's still in motion. In those is where you can easiest see the backward movement. There's one that's just slightly above the very center of the image that shows it pretty well.  The backward movement is at around the 7-8 o'clock point of the bald spot.  I wouldn't have expected it to reverse course like that. Neat!

With that frame rate (about 2e6/s), light will travel around 150m between frames. Find a tall antenna mast that has strobe lights on it, and film it on a foggy evening. You might be able to see the spherical shells of light expanding from the strobes scattering off the fog. How clear they would be depends on how fast a strobe flash actually starts and stops, though. The shape of the fog bank would make a difference too, you would prefer a wall of fog perpendicular to your point of view and clear air between you and the fog. Then again it might not be bright enough.

Hardened glass, wich means: Tempering puts the outer surfaces into compression and the interior into tension. Such stresses cause the glass, when broken, to shatter into small granular chunks instead of splintering into jagged shards as ordinary annealed glass does. The granular chunks are less likely to cause injury. Stle this last paragraph from wikipedia.

Commenting at 3:03 - That wave travels roughly 3cm per frame, or 0.03 meters per frame. It is being recorded at 82000fps, which means the wave travels 0.03m in 1/82000 seconds, which is  2460 m/s. Which is about the speed of sound in glass, which makes perfect sense.

Do lightning! (or a tesla coil, something with static electricity)  So how fast does it crack? Just eyeballing your font size of "Slow Mo Guys" I'd say the entire width is about 18 inches (big error bars here- I didn't go measuring pixels or anything). If it takes .000181s to move from left to right, as you say, that's about 2,526 m/s 5650 mph). And that is the crack moving up AND down not just exactly left to right, so it is even faster than that (maybe like 40%? taking the hypotenuse of some triangles up and down).  So maybe like 3 or 4 km/s...   However, my original speed ( 2500m/s) is about 2x faster than your findings in "How fast does glass crack? - The Slow Mo Guys" (2018) of 4578ft/s (1395m/s | 3100 mph). But I suspect you can be more accurate by simply measuring the 'exact' distance before hand. Cheers!

5:00  How fast is 100 nano-seconds? At a 100 nano-second shutter speed you would not to able to take a picture of something farther than 15 meters away from you. The light would simply not have enough time to travel the distance from your flash, to the object, and into the camera sensor. The light would not travel the total 30m distance for the duration of your shutter being open.

For those who are wondering what it happening: 1. Tempered Glass is constructed in such a way that the skin is put under tension as the glass cools. This tension gives the piece incredible strength against shattering when a distributed force is applied (like the sledgehammer) by deforming like a sheet flapping in the wind. The energy ripples across the glass, but the piece maintains it integrity.  2. BUT, if you SCRATCH the glass, that tension is released and the piece shatters under the strain.This shattering renders the glass into tiny little cubes. While these little bits are still sharp, you don't get the needle like, ultra thin, fines of glass that could shred skin.   It's odd, but both of these properties are the desired outcome of tempered glass. You don't want the glass to break just by someone putting their elbow, fist, or head through the glass, but when it does break, you want it forming shapes much safer than normal glass. (Trust me, I've worked with art glass for slumping and fusing. It's VERY EASY to cut oneself without knowing it, only to look down at all the blood on the table.)  Prince Rupert Drops demonstrate what goes into tempered glass.

I want a maximum framerate shot.  Recording at 1.75 million frames per second means that light (at about 300 million m/s) travels 170 meters per frame. In a fiber optic cable, light "only" travels at 200 million m/s, meaning 117 meters per frame.  In short, using this camera you could genuinely see light traveling, albeit in limited cases. A 3 km fiber optic cable would result in a 1 (recorded) second delay between the ends.

So, when I saw the 82kfps shot I began measuring... Presuming the spark plug chunk in that first crack was about an inch (25.4mm) long, each frame sees about half an inch (12.7mm) growth. This means the following: 42,000 inches per second. 3500 feet (1066.8 meters) per second. 2386 miles (3840 Km) per hour. All numbers are estimates based on length comparisons. Expect up to a 25% +/- inaccuracy. EDIT: On checking, that spark plug chunk may have been a bit longer which scales all these numbers up nearer the +25% inaccuracy.

That looks amazing, the computational power in that camera is mind boggling, it needs to refresh the image sensor at 500Khz, which means it'd have to be running a CPU that was at least twice or 3 times that speed, and RAM also on par with the CPU, every time it refreshes the sensor it needs to read, process, and send the individual pixel data to the RAM, where it's stored for playback and then saving.  That's SERIOUSLY fast.  Will probably take another 10 or 20 years to top this camera, but I'm keen

800p and 736p are NOT 720p lol. i don't get why people say things like this. 800p is its own specific thing, and you used to see it show up on laptops pretty often. 736p is another oddball one i've seen on older screens. 720p is the most common resolution for older "wide screen" monitors from the 2000's or 2010's. Maybe people lump them in because marketing just decided to start rounding numbers with "2k" and up? Or maybe its the whole 21:9 thing not being a real ratio? At any rate, 720p is literally 720 progressive scan vertical height, and nothing more or less. It could even mean a "standard def" 4:3 ratio screen.

*1:54** - **1:59** = 410'000 pictures.* :o 1280 x 736 = 942,080 pixels, but without the color dept I can't calculate the size in bytes for each frame, lets assume 8 bit (aka. 1 byte, 0-255) for each: Red, Green & Blue in each pixel, so: 1 pixel = 3 byte (or 3*8 bit = 24 bit) So, multiply the amount of pixels by 3, which is 942,080 * 3 =  2'826'240 bytes, which is 2.8 Mb per frame. times 410'000 frames: 410'000 * 2.8 = 1'148'000 Mb which is 1,148 TeraBytes in 5 seconds. 82'000 frames per second, at 2.8 Mb per frame: 82'000 * 2.8 = 229'600 Mb, or 229,6 Gigabyte per second. or about ~0,23 TeraByte per second.
